   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Valentine's Day</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Holiday observed on February 14
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       This article is about the romantic holiday and liturgical celebration. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Valentine's Day (disambiguation)">Valentine's Day (disambiguation)</a>.
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       "St. Valentine's Day" redirects here. For the Bing Crosby album, see <a href="/wiki/St._Valentine%27s_Day_(album)" title="St. Valentine's Day (album)">St. Valentine's Day (album)</a>.
      </div> 
      <p class="mw-empty-elt"> </p>

        </tr>
        <tr>
         <th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Type</th>
         <td class="infobox-data">Christian, romantic, cultural, commercial observance</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
         <th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Significance</th>
         <td class="infobox-data summary">Feast day of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Valentine" title="Saint Valentine">Saint Valentine</a>; the celebration of love and affection</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
         <th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Observances</th>
         <td class="infobox-data">Sending greeting cards and gifts, dating, church services, novenas</td>

           <li>July 30<br><i>(fixed by the Eastern Orthodox Church)</i></li>
          </ul> </td>
        </tr>

         <td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r992953826">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .navbox .navbar{display:block;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .navbox-title .navbar{float:left;text-align:left;margin-right:0.5em}</style>
          <div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini">
           <ul>

      </table> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ergersheim_NDAltbronn_46.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Ergersheim_NDAltbronn_46.JPG/220px-Ergersheim_NDAltbronn_46.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Ergersheim_NDAltbronn_46.JPG/330px-Ergersheim_NDAltbronn_46.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Ergersheim_NDAltbronn_46.JPG/440px-Ergersheim_NDAltbronn_46.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2941" data-file-height="4442"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ergersheim_NDAltbronn_46.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Saint_Valentine" title="Saint Valentine">Saint Valentine</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><b>Valentine's Day</b>, also called <b>Saint Valentine's Day</b> or the <b>Feast of Saint Valentine</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is celebrated annually on February 14.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> It originated as a Christian <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast day</a> honoring one or two early <a href="/wiki/Christian_martyrs" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian martyrs">Christian martyrs</a> named <a href="/wiki/Saint_Valentine" title="Saint Valentine">Saint Valentine</a> and, through later folk traditions, has become a significant cultural, religious, and commercial celebration of <a href="/wiki/Romance_(love)" title="Romance (love)">romance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romance_(love)" title="Romance (love)">love</a> in many regions of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Kithcart2013_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kithcart2013-3">[3]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There are a number of martyrdom stories associated with various Valentines connected to February 14,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> including an account of the imprisonment of Saint Valentine of Rome for ministering to Christians <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire">persecuted under the Roman Empire</a> in the third century.<sup id="cite_ref-Cooper2013_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooper2013-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ChryssidesWilkins2014_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChryssidesWilkins2014-6">[6]</a></sup> According to an early tradition, Saint Valentine restored sight to the blind daughter of his jailer.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball1993_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball1993-7">[7]</a></sup> Numerous later additions to the legend have better related it to the theme of love: an 18th-century embellishment to the legend claims he wrote the jailer's daughter a letter signed "Your Valentine" as a farewell before his execution;<sup id="cite_ref-Guiley2001_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guiley2001-8">[8]</a></sup> another addition posits that Saint Valentine performed weddings for Christian soldiers who were forbidden to marry.<sup id="cite_ref-ChryssidesWilkins2014_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChryssidesWilkins2014-6">[6]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Feast of Saint Valentine was established by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gelasius_I" title="Pope Gelasius I">Pope Gelasius I</a> in AD 496 to be celebrated on February 14 in honour of Saint Valentine of Rome, who died on that date in AD 269.<sup id="cite_ref-Butler1981_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butler1981-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ChanchreekJain2007_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChanchreekJain2007-10">[10]</a></sup> The day became associated with romantic love in the 14th and 15th centuries when notions of <a href="/wiki/Courtly_love" title="Courtly love">courtly love</a> flourished, apparently by association with the "<a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection_in_birds" title="Sexual selection in birds">lovebirds</a>" of early spring. In 18th-century England, it grew into an occasion in which couples expressed their love for each other by presenting flowers, offering confectionery, and sending greeting cards (known as "valentines"). Valentine's Day symbols that are used today include the heart-shaped outline, doves, and the figure of the winged <a href="/wiki/Cupid" title="Cupid">Cupid</a>. Since the 19th century, handwritten valentines have given way to mass-produced greeting cards.<sup id="cite_ref-Leigh_Eric_Schmidt_1993_pp._209–245_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leigh_Eric_Schmidt_1993_pp._209–245-11">[11]</a></sup> In Italy, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Valentine%27s_Key" title="Saint Valentine's Key">Saint Valentine's Keys</a> are given to lovers "as a romantic symbol and an invitation to unlock the giver's heart", as well as to children to ward off <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a> (called Saint Valentine's Malady).<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Saint Valentine's Day is not a public holiday in any country, although it is an official feast day in the Anglican Communion<sup id="cite_ref-Anglican_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anglican-13">[13]</a></sup> and the Lutheran Church.<sup id="cite_ref-Pfatteicher2008_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pfatteicher2008-14">[14]</a></sup> Many parts of the Eastern Orthodox Church also celebrate Saint Valentine's Day on July 6 in honor of Roman presbyter Saint Valentine, and on July 30 in honor of <a href="/wiki/Hieromartyr" title="Hieromartyr">Hieromartyr</a> Valentine, the Bishop of Interamna (modern <a href="/wiki/Terni" title="Terni">Terni</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading">

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Saint_Valentine">Saint Valentine</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Saint_Valentine" title="Saint Valentine">Saint Valentine</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:St-valentine_110921-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/St-valentine_110921-01.jpg/220px-St-valentine_110921-01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="346" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/St-valentine_110921-01.jpg/330px-St-valentine_110921-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/St-valentine_110921-01.jpg/440px-St-valentine_110921-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="764" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:St-valentine_110921-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Shrine of St. Valentine in <a href="/wiki/Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church" title="Whitefriar Street Carmelite Church">Whitefriar Street Carmelite Church</a> in Dublin, Ireland
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Numerous early Christian martyrs were named <a href="/wiki/Saint_Valentine" title="Saint Valentine">Valentine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The Valentines honored on February 14 are Valentine of Rome (<i>Valentinus presb. m. Romae</i>) and Valentine of Terni (<i>Valentinus ep. Interamnensis m. Romae</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Valentine of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> was a priest in Rome who was martyred in 269 and was added to the calendar of saints by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gelasius_I" title="Pope Gelasius I">Pope Gelasius I</a> in 496 and was buried on the <a href="/wiki/Via_Flaminia" title="Via Flaminia">Via Flaminia</a>. The relics of Saint Valentine were kept in the <a href="/wiki/Catacombs_of_San_Valentino" title="Catacombs of San Valentino">Church and Catacombs of San Valentino</a> in Rome, which "remained an important pilgrim site throughout the Middle Ages until the relics of St. Valentine were transferred to the church of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Prassede" title="Santa Prassede">Santa Prassede</a> during the pontificate of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_IV" title="Pope Nicholas IV">Nicholas IV</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Matilda_Webb_2001_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matilda_Webb_2001-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The flower-crowned skull of Saint Valentine is exhibited in the Basilica of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_in_Cosmedin" title="Santa Maria in Cosmedin">Santa Maria in Cosmedin</a>, Rome. Other relics are found at <a href="/wiki/Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church" title="Whitefriar Street Carmelite Church">Whitefriar Street Carmelite Church</a> in Dublin, Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Valentine of Terni became bishop of <a href="/wiki/Interamna_Nahars" class="mw-redirect" title="Interamna Nahars">Interamna</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Terni" title="Terni">Terni</a>, in central Italy) and is said to have been martyred during the persecution under Emperor <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a> in 273. He is buried on the Via Flaminia, but in a different location from Valentine of Rome. His relics are at the Basilica of Saint Valentine in Terni (<i>Basilica di San Valentino</i>). Professor Jack B. Oruch of the University of Kansas notes that "abstracts of the acts of the two saints were in nearly every church and monastery of Europe."<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Catholic_Encyclopedia" title="Catholic Encyclopedia">Catholic Encyclopedia</a></i> also speaks of a third saint named Valentine who was mentioned in early <a href="/wiki/Martyrology" title="Martyrology">martyrologies</a> under date of February 14. He was martyred in Africa with a number of companions, but nothing more is known about him.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> A relic claimed to be Saint Valentine of Terni's head was preserved in the abbey of <a href="/wiki/New_Minster,_Winchester" title="New Minster, Winchester">New Minster, Winchester</a>, and venerated.<sup id="cite_ref-ansgar_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar-23">[23]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>February 14 is celebrated as St. Valentine's Day in various <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denominations</a>; it has, for example, the rank of 'commemoration' in the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Church_of_England)" title="Calendar of saints (Church of England)">calendar of saints</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Anglican_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anglican-13">[13]</a></sup> In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast day</a> of Saint Valentine is also given in the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_Saints_(Lutheran)" class="mw-redirect" title="Calendar of Saints (Lutheran)">calendar of saints</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran Church">Lutheran Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pfatteicher2008_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pfatteicher2008-14">[14]</a></sup> However, in <a href="/wiki/Mysterii_Paschalis" title="Mysterii Paschalis">the 1969 revision</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Calendar_of_Saints" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Calendar of Saints">Roman Catholic Calendar of Saints</a>, the feast day of Saint Valentine on February 14 was removed from the <a href="/wiki/General_Roman_Calendar" title="General Roman Calendar">General Roman Calendar</a> and relegated to particular (local or even national) calendars for the following reason: "Though the memorial of Saint Valentine is ancient, it is left to particular calendars, since, apart from his name, nothing is known of Saint Valentine except that he was buried on the Via Flaminia on February 14."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The feast day is still celebrated in <a href="/wiki/Balzan" title="Balzan">Balzan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>) where relics of the saint are claimed to be found, and also throughout the world by <a href="/wiki/Traditionalist_Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditionalist Catholics">Traditionalist Catholics</a> who follow the older, pre-<a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a> calendar (see <a href="/wiki/General_Roman_Calendar_of_1960" title="General Roman Calendar of 1960">General Roman Calendar of 1960</a>). </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>, St. Valentine is recognized on <a href="/wiki/July_6_(Eastern_Orthodox_liturgics)" title="July 6 (Eastern Orthodox liturgics)">July 6</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Saint_Valentine" title="Saint Valentine">Saint Valentine</a>, the Roman presbyter, is honoured; in addition, the Eastern Orthodox Church observes the feast of Hieromartyr Valentine, Bishop of Interamna, on <a href="/wiki/July_30_(Eastern_Orthodox_liturgics)" title="July 30 (Eastern Orthodox liturgics)">July 30</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legends">Legends</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:St-valentine-baptizing-st-lucilla-jacopo-bassano.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/St-valentine-baptizing-st-lucilla-jacopo-bassano.jpg/220px-St-valentine-baptizing-st-lucilla-jacopo-bassano.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/St-valentine-baptizing-st-lucilla-jacopo-bassano.jpg/330px-St-valentine-baptizing-st-lucilla-jacopo-bassano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/St-valentine-baptizing-st-lucilla-jacopo-bassano.jpg/440px-St-valentine-baptizing-st-lucilla-jacopo-bassano.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="955"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:St-valentine-baptizing-st-lucilla-jacopo-bassano.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><i>St Valentine baptizing St Lucilla</i>, <a href="/wiki/Jacopo_Bassano" title="Jacopo Bassano">Jacopo Bassano</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>J.C. Cooper, in <i>The Dictionary of Christianity</i>, writes that Saint Valentine was "a priest of Rome who was imprisoned for succouring persecuted Christians."<sup id="cite_ref-JC_Cooper_2013_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JC_Cooper_2013-28">[28]</a></sup> Contemporary records of Saint Valentine were most probably destroyed during this <a href="/wiki/Diocletianic_Persecution" title="Diocletianic Persecution">Diocletianic Persecution</a> in the early 4th century.<sup id="cite_ref-oruch_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oruch-29">[29]</a></sup> In the 5th or 6th century, a work called <i>Passio Marii et Marthae</i> published a story of martyrdom for Saint Valentine of Rome, perhaps by borrowing tortures that happened to other saints, as was usual in the literature of that period. The same events are also found in <i><a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede's Martyrology</a></i>, which was compiled in the 8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-oruch_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oruch-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ansgar49_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar49-30">[30]</a></sup> It states that Saint Valentine was persecuted as a Christian and interrogated by <a href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Gothicus" title="Claudius Gothicus">Claudius II</a> in person. Claudius was impressed by Valentine and had a discussion with him, attempting to get him to convert to Roman <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">paganism</a> in order to save his life. Valentine refused and tried to convert Claudius to Christianity instead. Because of this, he was executed. Before his execution, he is reported to have performed a miracle by healing Julia, the blind daughter of his jailer Asterius. The jailer's daughter and his forty-six member <a href="/wiki/Household" title="Household">household</a> (family members and servants) came to believe in <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> and were <a href="/wiki/Baptized" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptized">baptized</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brewer1894_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brewer1894-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oruch_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oruch-29">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A later <i>Passio</i> repeated the legend, adding that <a href="/wiki/Pope_Julius_I" title="Pope Julius I">Pope Julius I</a> built a church over his sepulchre (it is a confusion with a 4th-century tribune called Valentino who donated land to build a church at a time when Julius was a Pope).<sup id="cite_ref-ansgar49_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar49-30">[30]</a></sup> The legend was picked up as fact by later martyrologies, starting with <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>'s martyrology in the 8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-ansgar49_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar49-30">[30]</a></sup> It was repeated in the 13th century, in <i><a href="/wiki/Golden_Legend" title="Golden Legend">The Golden Legend</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There is an additional embellishment to <i>The Golden Legend</i>, which according to Henry Ansgar Kelly, was added in the 18th century and widely repeated.<sup id="cite_ref-your_valentine_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-your_valentine-33">[33]</a></sup> On the evening before Valentine was to be <a href="/wiki/Executed" class="mw-redirect" title="Executed">executed</a>, he is supposed to have written the first "valentine" card himself, addressed to the daughter of his jailer Asterius, who was no longer blind, signing as "Your Valentine."<sup id="cite_ref-your_valentine_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-your_valentine-33">[33]</a></sup> The expression "From your Valentine" was later adopted by modern Valentine letters.<sup id="cite_ref-Ruth_Lee_1952_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruth_Lee_1952-34">[34]</a></sup> This legend has been published by both <a href="/wiki/American_Greetings" title="American Greetings">American Greetings</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_History_Channel" class="mw-redirect" title="The History Channel">The History Channel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Valentineanddisciples.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Valentineanddisciples.jpg/220px-Valentineanddisciples.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Valentineanddisciples.jpg/330px-Valentineanddisciples.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Valentineanddisciples.jpg/440px-Valentineanddisciples.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="459"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Valentineanddisciples.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Saint_Valentine" title="Saint Valentine">Saint Valentine of Terni</a> and his disciples
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/John_Foxe" title="John Foxe">John Foxe</a>, an English historian, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Carmelites" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of Carmelites">Order of Carmelites</a>, state that Saint Valentine was buried in the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Prassede" title="Santa Prassede">Church of Praxedes</a> in Rome, located near the cemetery of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Hippolytus" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Hippolytus">Saint Hippolytus</a>. This order says that according to legend, "Julia herself planted a pink-blossomed almond tree near his grave. Today, the almond tree remains a symbol of abiding love and friendship."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Another embellishment suggests that Saint Valentine performed clandestine <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_marriage" title="Christian views on marriage">Christian weddings</a> for soldiers who were forbidden to marry.<sup id="cite_ref-David_Harkness_1961_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_Harkness_1961-38">[38]</a></sup> The Roman Emperor Claudius II supposedly forbade this in order to grow his army, believing that married men did not make for good soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-David_Harkness_1961_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_Harkness_1961-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Max_Christensen_1997_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Max_Christensen_1997-39">[39]</a></sup> However, George Monger writes that this marriage ban was never issued and that Claudius II told his soldiers to take two or three women for themselves after his victory over the <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Monger2013_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monger2013-40">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to legend, in order "to remind these men of their vows and God's love, Saint Valentine is said to have cut hearts from parchment", giving them to these soldiers and <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire">persecuted Christians</a>, a possible origin of the widespread use of hearts on St. Valentine's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-Frank_Staff_1969_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frank_Staff_1969-41">[41]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Saint Valentine supposedly wore a purple <a href="/wiki/Amethyst" title="Amethyst">amethyst</a> ring, customarily worn on the hands of Christian <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a> with an image of <a href="/wiki/Cupid" title="Cupid">Cupid</a> engraved in it, a recognizable symbol associated with love that was legal under the Roman Empire;<sup id="cite_ref-Max_Christensen_1997_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Max_Christensen_1997-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMNH_1958_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMNH_1958-42">[42]</a></sup> Roman soldiers would recognize the ring and ask him to perform marriage for them.<sup id="cite_ref-Max_Christensen_1997_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Max_Christensen_1997-39">[39]</a></sup> Probably due to the association with Saint Valentine, amethyst has become the <a href="/wiki/Birthstone" title="Birthstone">birthstone</a> of February, which is thought to attract love.<sup id="cite_ref-Hesse2007_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hesse2007-43">[43]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_traditions">Folk traditions</span></h2> 
      <p>While the European folk traditions connected with Saint Valentine and St. Valentine's Day have become marginalized by the modern Anglo-American customs connecting the day with <a href="/wiki/Romantic_love" class="mw-redirect" title="Romantic love">romantic love</a>, there are some remaining associations connecting the saint with the advent of <a href="/wiki/Spring_(season)" title="Spring (season)">spring</a>. </p>
      <p>While the custom of sending cards, flowers, chocolates and other gifts originated in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a>, Valentine's Day still remains connected with various regional customs in England. In <a href="/wiki/Norfolk" title="Norfolk">Norfolk</a>, a character called 'Jack' Valentine knocks on the rear door of houses leaving sweets and presents for children. Although he was leaving treats, many children were scared of this mystical person.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, Saint Valentine or <a href="/wiki/Zdravko" title="Zdravko">Zdravko</a> was one of the saints of spring, the saint of good health and the patron of <a href="/wiki/Beekeepers" class="mw-redirect" title="Beekeepers">beekeepers</a> and pilgrims.<sup id="cite_ref-GG2008-02-15_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG2008-02-15-46">[46]</a></sup> A proverb says that "Saint Valentine brings the keys of roots". Plants and flowers start to grow on this day. It has been celebrated as the day when the first work in the vineyards and in the fields commences. It is also said that birds propose to each other or marry on that day. Another proverb says <i>"Valentin – prvi spomladin"</i> ("Valentine – the first spring saint"), as in some places (especially <a href="/wiki/White_Carniola" title="White Carniola">White Carniola</a>), Saint Valentine marks the beginning of spring.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Valentine's Day has only recently been celebrated as the day of love. The day of love was traditionally March 12, the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Gregory" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Gregory">Saint Gregory</a>'s day, or February 22, <a href="/wiki/Vincent_of_Saragossa" title="Vincent of Saragossa">Saint Vincent's Day</a>. The patron of love was <a href="/wiki/Anthony_of_Padua" title="Anthony of Padua">Saint Anthony</a>, whose day has been celebrated on June 13.<sup id="cite_ref-GG2008-02-15_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GG2008-02-15-46">[46]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Connection_with_romantic_love">Connection with romantic love</span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_ancient_origins">Possible ancient origins</span></h3> 
      <p>No evidence has been demonstrated to link St. Valentine's Day and the rites of the ancient Roman purification festival of <a href="/wiki/Lupercalia" title="Lupercalia">Lupercalia</a>, despite persistent and sometimes detailed claims by many authors to the contrary, nor to any otherwise unspecified Greco-Roman holiday supposed to have celebrated love or fertility.<sup id="cite_ref-ansgar_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[notes 1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BDForbes2015_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BDForbes2015-50">[49]</a></sup> The celebration of Saint Valentine is not known to have had any romantic connotations until <a href="/wiki/Chaucer" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaucer">Chaucer</a>'s poetry about "Valentine's Day" in the 14th century, some seven hundred years after celebration of Lupercalia is believed to have ceased.<sup id="cite_ref-oruch_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oruch-29">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a>, Lupercalia was observed February 13–15. It was a rite connected to purification and health, and had only slight connection to fertility (as a part of health) and none to love. Lupercalia was a festival local to the city of Rome. The more general Festival of <a href="/wiki/Juno_Februa" class="mw-redirect" title="Juno Februa">Juno Februa</a>, meaning "Juno the purifier" or "the chaste Juno", was celebrated on February 13–14. Pope <a href="/wiki/Gelasius_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Gelasius I">Gelasius I</a> (492–496) abolished Lupercalia. Some researchers have theorized that Gelasius I replaced Lupercalia with the celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Candlemas" title="Candlemas">Purification of the Blessed Virgin Mary</a> and claim a connection to the 14th century's connotations of romantic love, but there is no historical indication that he ever intended such a thing.<sup id="cite_ref-BDForbes2015_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BDForbes2015-50">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[notes 2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oruch_lupercalia_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oruch_lupercalia-52">[50]</a></sup> Also, the dates do not fit because at the time of Gelasius I, the feast was only celebrated in Jerusalem, and it was on February 14 only because Jerusalem placed the Nativity of Jesus (Christmas) on January 6.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[notes 3]</a></sup> Although it was called "Purification of the Blessed Virgin Mary", it also dealt with the presentation of Jesus at the temple.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-54">[51]</a></sup> Jerusalem's Purification of the Blessed Virgin Mary on February 14 became the <a href="/wiki/Presentation_of_Jesus_at_the_Temple" title="Presentation of Jesus at the Temple">Presentation of Jesus at the Temple</a> on February 2 as it was introduced to Rome and other places in the sixth century, after Gelasius I's time.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-54">[51]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Alban_Butler" title="Alban Butler">Alban Butler</a> in his <i>Lifes of the Principal Saints</i> (1756–1759) claimed without proof that men and women in Lupercalia drew names from a jar to make couples, and that modern Valentine's letters originated from this custom. In reality, this practice originated in the Middle Ages, with no link to Lupercalia, with men drawing the names of girls at random to couple with them. This custom was combated by priests, for example by <a href="/wiki/Frances_de_Sales" class="mw-redirect" title="Frances de Sales">Frances de Sales</a> around 1600, apparently by replacing it with a religious custom of girls drawing the names of apostles from the <a href="/wiki/Altar" title="Altar">altar</a>. However, this religious custom is recorded as soon as the 13th century in the life of Saint <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_Hungary" title="Elizabeth of Hungary">Elizabeth of Hungary</a>, so it could have a different origin.<sup id="cite_ref-ansgar_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar-23">[23]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Chaucer_Hoccleve.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Chaucer_Hoccleve.png/220px-Chaucer_Hoccleve.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Chaucer_Hoccleve.png/330px-Chaucer_Hoccleve.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Chaucer_Hoccleve.png/440px-Chaucer_Hoccleve.png 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="384"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Chaucer_Hoccleve.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hoccleve" title="Thomas Hoccleve">Thomas Hoccleve</a> (1412)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><span class="anchor" id="Chaucer's_love"></span> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Chaucer.27s_Parliament_of_Fowls"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chaucer's_Parliament_of_Fowls">Chaucer's <i>Parliament of Fowls</i></span></h3> 
      <p>The first recorded association of Valentine's Day with romantic love is believed to be in the <i><a href="/wiki/Parlement_of_Foules" title="Parlement of Foules">Parliament of Fowls</a></i> (1382) by <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Dream_vision" title="Dream vision">dream vision</a> portraying a parliament for birds to choose their mates.<sup id="cite_ref-oruch_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oruch-29">[29]</a></sup> Honoring the first anniversary of the engagement of fifteen-year-old King <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">Richard II of England</a> to fifteen-year-old <a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Bohemia" title="Anne of Bohemia">Anne of Bohemia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ansgar2001_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar2001-55">[52]</a></sup> Chaucer wrote (in <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>): </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>"For this was on seynt Valentynes day<br>Whan every foul cometh there to chese his make<br>Of every kynde that men thynke may<br>And that so huge a noyse gan they make<br>That erthe, and eyr, and tre, and every lake<br>So ful was, that unethe was there space<br>For me to stonde, so ful was al the place."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>In modern English: </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>"For this was on Saint Valentine's Day<br>When every bird comes there to choose his match<br>Of every kind that men may think of<br>And that so huge a noise they began to make<br>That earth and air and tree and every lake<br>Was so full, that not easily was there space<br>For me to stand—so full was all the place."</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Readers have uncritically assumed that Chaucer was referring to February 14 as Valentine's Day. Henry Ansgar Kelly has observed that Chaucer might have had in mind the feast day of St. Valentine of Genoa, an early <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Genoa" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Genoa">bishop of Genoa</a> who died around AD 307; it was probably celebrated on 3 May.<sup id="cite_ref-ansgar2001_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar2001-55">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup> A treaty providing for Richard II and Anne's marriage, the subject of the poem, was signed on May 2, 1381.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Jack B. Oruch notes that <a href="/wiki/March_equinox" title="March equinox">the date on which spring begins</a> has changed since Chaucer's time because of the <a href="/wiki/Axial_precession" title="Axial precession">precession of the equinoxes</a> and the introduction of the more accurate <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a> only in 1582. On the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a> in use in Chaucer's time, February 14 would have fallen on the date now called February 23, a time when some birds have started mating and nesting in England.<sup id="cite_ref-oruch_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oruch-29">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Chaucer's <i>Parliament of Fowls</i> refers to a supposedly established tradition, but there is no record of such a tradition before Chaucer. The speculative derivation of sentimental customs from the distant past began with 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Antiquary" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiquary">antiquaries</a>, notably <a href="/wiki/Alban_Butler" title="Alban Butler">Alban Butler</a>, the author of <i>Butler's Lives of Saints</i>, and have been perpetuated even by respectable modern scholars. Most notably, "the idea that Valentine's Day customs perpetuated those of the Roman <a href="/wiki/Lupercalia" title="Lupercalia">Lupercalia</a> has been accepted uncritically and repeated, in various forms, up to the present".<sup id="cite_ref-ansgar_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oruch_539_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oruch_539-61">[58]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Three other authors who made poems about <a href="/wiki/Birds_mating" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds mating">birds mating</a> on St. Valentine's Day around the same years: <a href="/wiki/Otton_de_Grandson" class="mw-redirect" title="Otton de Grandson">Otton de Grandson</a> from Savoy, <a href="/wiki/John_Gower" title="John Gower">John Gower</a> from England, and a knight called Pardo from Valencia. Chaucer most probably predated all of them; but due to the difficulty of dating medieval works, it is not possible to ascertain which of the four may have influenced the others.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Court_of_love">Court of love</span></h3> 
      <p>The earliest description of February 14 as an annual celebration of love appears in the <i>Charter of the Court of Love</i>. The charter, allegedly issued by <a href="/wiki/Charles_VI_of_France" title="Charles VI of France">Charles VI of France</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mantes-la-Jolie" title="Mantes-la-Jolie">Mantes-la-Jolie</a> in 1400, describes lavish festivities to be attended by several members of the royal court, including a feast, amorous song and poetry competitions, <a href="/wiki/Jousting" title="Jousting">jousting</a> and dancing.<sup id="cite_ref-ansgar_court_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar_court-63">[60]</a></sup> Amid these festivities, the attending ladies would hear and rule on disputes from lovers.<sup id="cite_ref-goodrich_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goodrich-64">[61]</a></sup> No other record of the court exists, and none of those named in the charter were present at Mantes except Charles's queen, <a href="/wiki/Isabeau_of_Bavaria" title="Isabeau of Bavaria">Isabeau of Bavaria</a>, who may well have imagined it all while waiting out a plague.<sup id="cite_ref-ansgar_court_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ansgar_court-63">[60]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Valentine_poetry">Valentine poetry</span></h3> 
      <p>The earliest surviving valentine is a 15th-century <a href="/wiki/Rondeau_(poetry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rondeau (poetry)">rondeau</a> written by <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Duke_of_Orl%C3%A9ans" title="Charles, Duke of Orléans">Charles, Duke of Orléans</a> to his wife, which commences. </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>"Je suis desja d'amour tanné<br> Ma tres doulce Valentinée..."</p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        — <cite>Charles d'Orléans, Rondeau VI, lines 1–2<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup></cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p> At the time, the duke was being held in the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> following his capture at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agincourt" title="Battle of Agincourt">Battle of Agincourt</a>, 1415.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The earliest surviving valentines in English appear to be those in the <i><a href="/wiki/Paston_Letters" title="Paston Letters">Paston Letters</a></i>, written in 1477 by Margery Brewes to her future husband John Paston "my right well-beloved Valentine".<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Valentine's Day is mentioned ruefully by Ophelia in <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i> (1600–1601): </p> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942">
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>"To-morrow is Saint Valentine's day,<br>All in the morning betime,<br>And I a maid at your window,<br>To be your Valentine.<br>Then up he rose, and donn'd his clothes,<br>And dupp'd the chamber-door;<br>Let in the maid, that out a maid<br>Never departed more."</p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        — <cite>William Shakespeare, <i>Hamlet</i>, Act IV, Scene 5</cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/John_Donne" title="John Donne">John Donne</a> used the legend of the marriage of the birds as the starting point for his <a href="/wiki/Epithalamion" class="mw-redirect" title="Epithalamion">epithalamion</a> celebrating the marriage of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_Bohemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth of Bohemia">Elizabeth</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James I of England</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_V,_Elector_Palatine" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick V, Elector Palatine">Frederick V, Elector Palatine</a>, on Valentine's Day: </p> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942">
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>"Hayle Bishop Valentine whose day this is<br> </p>
       <p>All the Ayre is thy Diocese<br> And all the chirping Queristers<br> And other birds ar thy parishioners<br> Thou marryest every yeare<br> The Lyrick Lark, and the graue whispering Doue,<br> The Sparrow that neglects his life for loue,<br> The houshold bird with the redd stomacher<br> Thou makst the Blackbird speede as soone,<br> As doth the Goldfinch, or the Halcyon<br> The Husband Cock lookes out and soone is spedd<br> And meets his wife, which brings her feather-bed.<br> This day more cheerfully than ever shine<br> </p>
       <p> This day which might inflame thy selfe old Valentine."</p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        — <cite>John Donne, <i>Epithalamion Vpon Frederick Count Palatine and the Lady Elizabeth marryed on St. Valentines day</i></cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The verse "<a href="/wiki/Roses_are_red" class="mw-redirect" title="Roses are red">Roses are red</a>" echoes conventions traceable as far back as <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>'s epic <i><a href="/wiki/The_Faerie_Queene" title="The Faerie Queene">The Faerie Queene</a></i> (1590): </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>"She bath'd with roses red, and violets blew,<br> And all the sweetest flowres, that in the forrest grew."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The modern cliché Valentine's Day poem can be found in the collection of English nursery rhymes <i><a href="/wiki/Gammer_Gurton%27s_Garland" class="mw-redirect" title="Gammer Gurton's Garland">Gammer Gurton's Garland</a></i> (1784): </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>"The rose is red, the violet's blue,<br> </p>
       <p>The honey's sweet, and so are you.<br> Thou art my love and I am thine;<br> I drew thee to my Valentine:<br> The lot was cast and then I drew,<br> </p>
       <p> And Fortune said it shou'd be you."<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">Modern times</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Card;_valentine_card_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Card%3B_valentine_card_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Card%3B_valentine_card_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Card%3B_valentine_card_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Card%3B_valentine_card_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Card%3B_valentine_card_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Card%3B_valentine_card_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3193" data-file-height="4001"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Card;_valentine_card_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>An English Victorian era Valentine card located in the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_London" title="Museum of London">Museum of London</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1797, a British publisher issued <i>The Young Man's Valentine Writer</i>, which contained scores of suggested sentimental <a href="/wiki/Verse_(poetry)" title="Verse (poetry)">verses</a> for the young lover unable to compose his own. Printers had already begun producing a limited number of cards with verses and sketches, called "mechanical valentines." Paper Valentines became so popular in England in the early 19th century that they were assembled in factories. Fancy Valentines were made with real lace and ribbons, with paper lace introduced in the mid-19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> In 1835, 60,000 Valentine cards were sent by post in the United Kingdom, despite postage being expensive.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/Penny_Post#United_Kingdom" title="Penny Post">reduction in postal rates</a> following <a href="/wiki/Rowland_Hill_(postal_reformer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rowland Hill (postal reformer)">Sir Rowland Hill</a>'s postal reforms with the 1840 invention of the postage stamp (<a href="/wiki/Penny_Black" title="Penny Black">Penny Black</a>) saw the number of Valentines posted increase, with 400,000 sent just one year after its invention, and ushered in the less personal but easier practice of mailing Valentines.<sup id="cite_ref-Vincent_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vincent-73">[70]</a></sup> That made it possible for the first time to exchange cards anonymously, which is taken as the reason for the sudden appearance of racy verse in an era otherwise prudishly <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> Production increased, "Cupid's Manufactory" as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> termed it, with over 3,000 women employed in manufacturing.<sup id="cite_ref-Vincent_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vincent-73">[70]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Laura_Seddon_Greeting_Card_Collection" title="Laura Seddon Greeting Card Collection">Laura Seddon Greeting Card Collection</a> at <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Metropolitan_University" title="Manchester Metropolitan University">Manchester Metropolitan University</a> gathers 450 Valentine's Day cards dating from early nineteenth century Britain, printed by the major publishers of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> The collection appears in Seddon's book <i>Victorian Valentines</i> (1996).<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Red_Roses_(6862116332).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Red_Roses_%286862116332%29.jpg/170px-Red_Roses_%286862116332%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Red_Roses_%286862116332%29.jpg/255px-Red_Roses_%286862116332%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Red_Roses_%286862116332%29.jpg/340px-Red_Roses_%286862116332%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="951" data-file-height="1417"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Red_Roses_(6862116332).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Flowers, such as red roses (pictured), are often sent on Valentine's Day.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the United States, the first mass-produced Valentines of embossed paper lace were produced and sold shortly after 1847 by <a href="/wiki/Esther_Howland" title="Esther Howland">Esther Howland</a> (1828–1904) of <a href="/wiki/Worcester,_Massachusetts" title="Worcester, Massachusetts">Worcester, Massachusetts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-american_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american-77">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-devereux_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-devereux-78">[75]</a></sup> Her father operated a large book and stationery store, but Howland took her inspiration from an English Valentine she had received from a business associate of her father.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TRE_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TRE-80">[77]</a></sup> Intrigued with the idea of making similar Valentines, Howland began her business by importing paper lace and floral decorations from England.<sup id="cite_ref-TRE_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TRE-80">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> A writer in <i>Graham's American Monthly</i> observed in 1849, "Saint Valentine's Day&nbsp;... is becoming, nay it has become, a national holyday."<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> The English practice of sending Valentine's cards was established enough to feature as a plot device in <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Gaskell" title="Elizabeth Gaskell">Elizabeth Gaskell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Harrison%27s_Confessions" title="Mr. Harrison's Confessions">Mr. Harrison's Confessions</a></i> (1851): "I burst in with my explanations: 'The valentine I know nothing about.' 'It is in your handwriting', said he coldly."<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> Since 2001, the Greeting Card Association has been giving an annual "Esther Howland Award for a Greeting Card Visionary".<sup id="cite_ref-devereux_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-devereux-78">[75]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since the 19th century, handwritten notes have given way to mass-produced greeting cards.<sup id="cite_ref-Leigh_Eric_Schmidt_1993_pp._209–245_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leigh_Eric_Schmidt_1993_pp._209–245-11">[11]</a></sup> In the UK, just under half of the population spend money on their Valentines, and around £1.9&nbsp;billion was spent in 2015 on cards, flowers, chocolates and other gifts.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> The mid-19th century Valentine's Day trade was a harbinger of further commercialized holidays in the U.S. to follow.<sup id="cite_ref-Leigh_Eric_Schmidt_1991_pp_890–98_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leigh_Eric_Schmidt_1991_pp_890–98-85">[82]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg/170px-Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg/255px-Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg/340px-Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg 2x" data-file-width="751" data-file-height="736"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A gift box of chocolates, which is a common gift for Valentine's Day
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1868, the British chocolate company <a href="/wiki/Cadbury" title="Cadbury">Cadbury</a> created Fancy Boxes – a decorated box of chocolates – in the shape of a heart for Valentine's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-Chocolates_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chocolates-86">[83]</a></sup> Boxes of filled chocolates quickly became associated with the holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-Chocolates_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chocolates-86">[83]</a></sup> In the second half of the 20th century, the practice of exchanging cards was extended to all manner of gifts, such as giving <a href="/wiki/Jewelry" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewelry">jewelry</a>. </p>
      <p>The U.S. Greeting Card Association estimates that approximately 190 million valentines are sent each year in the US. Half of those valentines are given to family members other than husband or wife, usually to children. When the valentine-exchange cards made in school activities are included the figure goes up to 1&nbsp;billion, and teachers become the people receiving the most valentines.<sup id="cite_ref-american_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american-77">[74]</a></sup> The average valentine's spending has increased every year in the U.S, from $108 a person in 2010 to $131 in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-plu.edu_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plu.edu-87">[84]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The rise of Internet popularity at the turn of the millennium is creating new traditions. Millions of people use, every year, digital means of creating and sending Valentine's Day greeting messages such as <a href="/wiki/E-cards" class="mw-redirect" title="E-cards">e-cards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Love_coupons" class="mw-redirect" title="Love coupons">love coupons</a> or printable greeting cards. An estimated 15 million e-valentines were sent in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-american_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american-77">[74]</a></sup> Valentine's Day is considered by some to be a <a href="/wiki/Hallmark_holiday" title="Hallmark holiday">Hallmark holiday</a> due to its commercialization.<sup id="cite_ref-wp_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wp-88">[85]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the modern era, liturgically, the <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran Church">Lutheran Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a> have a service for St. Valentine's Day (the Feast of St. Valentine), which includes the optional rite of the <a href="/wiki/Wedding_vow_renewal_ceremony" title="Wedding vow renewal ceremony">renewal of marriage vows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup> In 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Bishops%27_Conference_of_England_and_Wales" title="Catholic Bishops' Conference of England and Wales">Catholic Bishops of England and Wales</a> established a <a href="/wiki/Novena" title="Novena">novena</a> prayer "to support single people seeking a spouse ahead of St Valentine's Day."<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Celebration_and_status_worldwide">Celebration and status worldwide</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:CWAC_Valentine%27s_Day_1944_(4112917253).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/CWAC_Valentine%27s_Day_1944_%284112917253%29.jpg/220px-CWAC_Valentine%27s_Day_1944_%284112917253%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/CWAC_Valentine%27s_Day_1944_%284112917253%29.jpg/330px-CWAC_Valentine%27s_Day_1944_%284112917253%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/CWAC_Valentine%27s_Day_1944_%284112917253%29.jpg/440px-CWAC_Valentine%27s_Day_1944_%284112917253%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="583"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:CWAC_Valentine%27s_Day_1944_(4112917253).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Canadian couple (she, a member of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Women%27s_Army_Corps" title="Canadian Women's Army Corps">Canadian Women's Army Corps</a>; he, a member of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian Air Force">Canadian Air Force</a>) chalking hearts onto a tree on Valentine's Day 1944
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Valentine's Day customs – sending greeting cards (known as “valentines”), offering confectionary and presenting flowers – developed in early modern England and spread throughout the <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">English-speaking world</a> in the 19th century. In the later 20th and early 21st centuries, these customs spread to other countries, like those of <a href="/wiki/Halloween" title="Halloween">Hallowe'en</a>, or than aspects of <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>, (such as <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa Claus</a>). </p>
      <p>Valentine's Day is celebrated in many <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asian</a> countries with <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singaporeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Koreans</a> spending the most money on Valentine's gifts.<sup id="cite_ref-showbizandstyle.inquirer.net_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-showbizandstyle.inquirer.net-92">[89]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span></h4> 
      <p>In most <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin American</a> countries, for example, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup> Mexico,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, Saint Valentine's Day is known as <i>Día de los Enamorados</i> (day of lovers)<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup> or as <i>Día del Amor y la Amistad</i> (Day of Love and Friendship). It is also common to see people perform "acts of appreciation" for their friends.<sup id="cite_ref-Levine2012_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levine2012-96">[93]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a> it is known as the "Día del Cariño" (Affection Day).<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup> Some countries, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup> have a tradition called <i>Amigo secreto</i> ("Secret friend"), which is a game similar to the <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a> tradition of <a href="/wiki/Secret_Santa" title="Secret Santa">Secret Santa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Levine2012_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levine2012-96">[93]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazil">Brazil</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dia_dos_Namorados" title="Dia dos Namorados">Dia dos Namorados</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Dia_dos_Namorados" title="Dia dos Namorados">Dia dos Namorados</a></i> (lit. "Lovers' Day", or "Boyfriends'/Girlfriends' Day") is celebrated on June 12, probably because that is the day before <a href="/wiki/Anthony_of_Padua" title="Anthony of Padua">Saint Anthony</a>'s day, known there as the <i>marriage saint</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup> when traditionally many single women perform popular rituals, called <i>simpatias</i>, in order to find a good husband or boyfriend. Couples exchange gifts, chocolates, cards, and flower bouquets. The February 14 Valentine's Day is not celebrated at all because it usually falls too little before or too little after the <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Carnival" title="Brazilian Carnival">Brazilian Carnival</a><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup> – that can fall anywhere from early February to early March and lasts almost a week. Because of the absence of Valentine's Day and due to the celebrations of the Carnivals, Brazil was recommended by <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i> as a tourist destination during February for Western singles who want to get away from the holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colombia">Colombia</span></h4> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> celebrates <i>Día del amor y la amistad</i> on the third Saturday in September instead.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup> <i>Amigo Secreto</i> is also popular there.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Necco-Candy-SweetHearts.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Necco-Candy-SweetHearts.jpg/220px-Necco-Candy-SweetHearts.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Necco-Candy-SweetHearts.jpg/330px-Necco-Candy-SweetHearts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Necco-Candy-SweetHearts.jpg/440px-Necco-Candy-SweetHearts.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3400" data-file-height="2640"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Necco-Candy-SweetHearts.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Conversation_hearts" class="mw-redirect" title="Conversation hearts">Conversation hearts</a>, candies with messages on them, are strongly associated with Valentine's Day.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Valentine_dance,_school_(2898490693).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Valentine_dance%2C_school_%282898490693%29.jpg/220px-Valentine_dance%2C_school_%282898490693%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Valentine_dance%2C_school_%282898490693%29.jpg/330px-Valentine_dance%2C_school_%282898490693%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Valentine_dance%2C_school_%282898490693%29.jpg/440px-Valentine_dance%2C_school_%282898490693%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="585"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Valentine_dance,_school_(2898490693).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a> Valentine's school dance, Richmond, Virginia 1956
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the United States, about 190 million Valentine's Day cards are sent each year, not including the hundreds of millions of cards school children exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Valentine's Day is a major source of economic activity, with total <a href="/wiki/Cost" title="Cost">expenditures</a> in 2017 topping $18.2&nbsp;billion in 2017, or over $136 per person.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup> This is an increase from $108 per person in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-plu.edu_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plu.edu-87">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</span></h4> 
      <p>In the Afghan <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a>, love is often expressed through <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a>. Some new generation budding poets like Ramin Mazhar, Mahtab Sahel are expressing themselves through poetry using Valentine's Day expressing concerns on any likelihood of erosion of freedoms. In their political comment they defy fear by saying '<i>I kiss you amid the Taliban'</i><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/The_Cowherd_and_the_Weaver_Girl" title="The Cowherd and the Weaver Girl">The Cowherd and the Weaver Girl</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In Chinese, Valentine's Day is called lovers' festival (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">情人节</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">情人節</span>; <a href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Mandarin">Mandarin</a>: <i>Qīng Rén Jié</i>; <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a>: <i>Chêng Lîn Chiat</i>; <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cantonese language">Cantonese</a>: <i>Chìhng Yàhn Jit</i>; <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Shanghainese language">Shanghainese</a> <i>Xin Yin Jiq</i>). The "Chinese Valentine's Day" is the <a href="/wiki/Qixi_Festival" title="Qixi Festival">Qixi Festival</a> (meaning "The Night of Sevens" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">七夕</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Qi Xi</span></i>)), celebrated on the seventh day of the seventh month of the lunar calendar. According to the legend, the <a href="/wiki/Altair" title="Altair">Cowherd star</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">Weaver Maid star</a> are normally separated by the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> (silvery river) but are allowed to meet by crossing it on the 7th day of the 7th month of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calendar" title="Chinese calendar">Chinese calendar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In recent years, celebrating <a href="/wiki/White_Day" title="White Day">White Day</a> has also become fashionable among some young people.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day_in_India" title="Valentine's Day in India">Valentine's Day in India</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In India, in antiquity, there was a tradition of adoring <a href="/wiki/Kamadeva" title="Kamadeva">Kamadeva</a>, the lord of love; exemplificated by the erotic carvings in the <a href="/wiki/Khajuraho_Group_of_Monuments" title="Khajuraho Group of Monuments">Khajuraho Group of Monuments</a> and by the writing of the <i><a href="/wiki/Kamasutra" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamasutra">Kamasutra</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-rana_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rana-109">[106]</a></sup> This tradition was lost around the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_India" title="Medieval India">Middle Ages</a>, when Kamadeva was no longer celebrated, and public displays of sexual affection became frowned upon.<sup id="cite_ref-rana_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rana-109">[106]</a></sup> This repression of public affections began to loosen in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Valentine's Day celebrations did not catch on in India until around 1992. It was spread due to the programs in commercial TV channels, such as <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a>, dedicated radio programs, and love letter competitions, in addition to an economical liberalization that allowed the explosion of the valentine card industry.<sup id="cite_ref-rana_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rana-109">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-derne_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derne-111">[108]</a></sup> The celebration has caused a sharp change on how people have been displaying their affection in public since the Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-rana_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rana-109">[106]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>When we give attention to Valentine's Day we find 68%, including Hindu,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup> Muslim<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup> and Christian of people of India not in support of Valentine's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In modern times, Hindu and Islamic<sup id="cite_ref-monger_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monger-115">[112]</a></sup> traditionalists have considered the holiday to be cultural contamination from the West, a result of globalization in India.<sup id="cite_ref-rana_109-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rana-109">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-derne_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derne-111">[108]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shiv_Sena" title="Shiv Sena">Shiv Sena</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sangh_Parivar" title="Sangh Parivar">Sangh Parivar</a> have asked their followers to shun the holiday and the "public admission of love" because of them being "alien to Indian culture".<sup id="cite_ref-sandbrook_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandbrook-116">[113]</a></sup> Although these protests are organized by political elites, the protesters themselves are middle-class Hindu men who fear that the globalization will destroy the traditions in their society: <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">arranged marriages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindu_joint_family" title="Hindu joint family">Hindu joint families</a>, <a href="/wiki/Housewife#In_India_and_Pakistan" title="Housewife">full-time mothers</a>, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-derne_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derne-111">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-monger_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monger-115">[112]</a></sup> Despite these obstacles, Valentine's Day is becoming increasingly popular in India.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcanger_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcanger-117">[114]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Valentine's Day has been strongly criticized from a <a href="/wiki/Postcolonial" class="mw-redirect" title="Postcolonial">postcolonial</a> perspective by intellectuals from the Indian left. The holiday is regarded as a front for "Western imperialism", "<a href="/wiki/Neocolonialism" title="Neocolonialism">neocolonialism</a>", and "the exploitation of working classes through <a href="/wiki/Commercialism" title="Commercialism">commercialism</a> by <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational corporations</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup> It is claimed that as a result of Valentine's Day, the <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working classes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rural_poverty" title="Rural poverty">rural poor</a> become more disconnected socially, politically, and geographically from the hegemonic <a href="/wiki/Capitalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Capitalist">capitalist</a> power structure. They also criticize mainstream media attacks on Indians opposed to Valentine's Day as a form of <a href="/wiki/Demonization" title="Demonization">demonization</a> that is designed and derived to further the Valentine's Day agenda.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup> Right wing <a href="/wiki/Hindu_nationalism" title="Hindu nationalism">Hindu nationalists</a> are also hostile. In February 2012, <a href="/wiki/Subash_Chouhan" title="Subash Chouhan">Subash Chouhan</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bajrang_Dal" title="Bajrang Dal">Bajrang Dal</a> warned couples that "They cannot kiss or hug in public places. Our activists will beat them up".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[118]</a></sup> He said "We are not against love, but we criticize vulgar exhibition of love at public places".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day_in_Iran" title="Valentine's Day in Iran">Valentine's Day in Iran</a>
      </div>
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Sepand%C4%81rmazg%C4%81n" title="Sepandārmazgān">Sepandārmazgān</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Iranians, particularly in cities, have become familiar with Valentine's Day for over a decade and during this short time, it has become popular among the youth and also some elders. In the 2010s, the celebration of Valentine's Day became common in wealthy families and then spread among other levels of society.<sup id="cite_ref-ولنتاین_تهاجم_یا_تهدید_فرهنگی_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ولنتاین_تهاجم_یا_تهدید_فرهنگی-123">[120]</a></sup> Specialists in the country say that banning Valentine's Day would encourage the youth more towards it.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup> Although Valentine's Day is not accepted/approved by any institution or rule in the country and has no official status, it's highly accepted among a large part of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[122]</a></sup> One of the reasons for Valentine's Day breaking out into the general population in Iran is the change in relations between the sexes, which are no longer bound to marriage for two decades.<sup id="cite_ref-ولنتاین_تهاجم_یا_تهدید_فرهنگی_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ولنتاین_تهاجم_یا_تهدید_فرهنگی-123">[120]</a></sup> </p>
