      <p>In the first part of the 21st century, the celebration of Valentine's Day in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> has been harshly criticized by Islamic teachers who see the celebrations as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Islamic_culture" title="Islamic culture">Islamic culture</a>. In 2011, the Iranian printing works owners' union issued a directive banning the printing and distribution of any goods promoting the holiday, including <a href="/wiki/Greeting_card" title="Greeting card">cards</a>, gifts, and <a href="/wiki/Teddy_bear" title="Teddy bear">teddy bears</a>. "Printing and producing any goods related to this day including posters, boxes and cards emblazoned with hearts or half-hearts, red <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">roses</a> and any activities promoting this day are banned&nbsp;... Outlets that violate this will be legally dealt with", the union warned.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[124]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2016, local media reported that police had informed coffee shops and ice cream parlours in Tehran that they would be guilty of committing a crime if they encouraged "decadent Western culture through Valentine's Day rituals".<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[125]</a></sup> Under Iran's Islamic law, unmarried couples are not allowed to mingle. That is why each year the printing works owners’ union issues an instruction on the ban of Valentine's Day, imposed by Iranian authorities which denies them from selling gifts such as cards, and boxes with the symbols of hearts and red roses.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[126]</a></sup> Iran says it is cracking down on Valentine's Day celebrations and shops engaging in them will be guilty of a crime.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup> In recent years Iranian authorities have forbidden Valentine's celebrations, calling the holiday a “decadent Western custom” and threatening shops and restaurants with prosecution if they sell Valentine's Day gifts.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[128]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In Iran, the <a href="/wiki/Sepandarmazgan" class="mw-redirect" title="Sepandarmazgan">Sepandarmazgan</a>, or Esfandegan, is a festival where people express love towards their mothers and wives, and it is also a celebration of earth in ancient Persian culture. It has been progressively forgotten in favor of the Western celebration of Valentine's Day. The Association of Iran's Cultural and Natural Phenomena has been trying since 2006 to make Sepandarmazgan a national holiday on February 17, in order to replace the Western holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Israel">Israel</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, the Jewish tradition of <a href="/wiki/Tu_B%27Av" title="Tu B'Av">Tu B'Av</a> has been revived and transformed into the Jewish equivalent of Valentine's Day. It is celebrated on the 15th day of the month of <a href="/wiki/Av" title="Av">Av</a> (usually in late August). In ancient times girls would wear white dresses and dance in the vineyards, where the boys would be waiting for them (<a href="/wiki/Mishna" class="mw-redirect" title="Mishna">Mishna</a> Taanith end of Chapter 4). Today, Tu B'Av is celebrated as a second holiday of love by secular people (along with Valentine's Day), and it shares many of the customs associated with Saint Valentine's Day in western societies. In modern Israeli culture Tu B'Av is a popular day to proclaim love, propose marriage, and give gifts like cards or flowers.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span></h4> 
      <p>In Japan, <a href="/wiki/Morozoff_Ltd." title="Morozoff Ltd.">Morozoff Ltd.</a> introduced the holiday for the first time in 1936, when it ran an advertisement aimed at foreigners. Later, in 1953, it began promoting the giving of heart-shaped chocolates; other Japanese confectionery companies followed suit thereafter. In 1958, the <a href="/wiki/Isetan" title="Isetan">Isetan</a> <a href="/wiki/Department_stores_in_Japan" title="Department stores in Japan">department store</a> ran a "Valentine sale". Further campaigns during the 1960s popularized the custom.<sup id="cite_ref-japantimes_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-japantimes-134">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rupp_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rupp-135">[132]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The custom that only women give chocolates to men may have originated from the translation error of a chocolate-company executive during the initial campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-jasgp_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jasgp-136">[133]</a></sup> In particular, <a href="/wiki/Office_lady" title="Office lady">office ladies</a> give chocolate to their co-workers. Unlike western countries, gifts such as greeting cards,<sup id="cite_ref-jasgp_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jasgp-136">[133]</a></sup> candies, <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Dating" title="Dating">dinner dates</a><sup id="cite_ref-yoshimura_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoshimura-137">[134]</a></sup> are uncommon, and most of the gifts-related activity is about giving the right amount of chocolate to each person.<sup id="cite_ref-jasgp_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jasgp-136">[133]</a></sup> Japanese chocolate companies make half their annual sales during this time of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-jasgp_136-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jasgp-136">[133]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Many women feel obliged to give chocolates to all male co-workers, except when the day falls on a Sunday, a holiday. This is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Giri_choco" title="Giri choco">giri-choko</a></i> (義理チョコ), from <i><a href="/wiki/Giri_(Japanese)" title="Giri (Japanese)">giri</a></i> ("obligation") and <i>choko</i>, ("chocolate"), with unpopular co-workers receiving only "ultra-obligatory" <i>chō-giri choko</i> cheap chocolate. This contrasts with <i><a href="/wiki/Honmei_choco" title="Honmei choco">honmei-choko</a></i> (本命チョコ, lit. "true feeling chocolate"), chocolate given to a loved one. Friends, especially girls, may exchange chocolate referred to as <i>tomo-choko</i> (友チョコ); from <i>tomo</i> meaning "friend".<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 1980s, the Japanese National Confectionery Industry Association launched a successful campaign to make March 14 a "reply day", where men are expected to return the favour to those who gave them chocolates on Valentine's Day, calling it <a href="/wiki/White_Day" title="White Day">White Day</a> for the color of the chocolates being offered. A previous failed attempt to popularize this celebration had been done by a <a href="/wiki/Marshmallow" title="Marshmallow">marshmallow</a> manufacturer who wanted men to return marshmallows to women.<sup id="cite_ref-japantimes_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-japantimes-134">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rupp_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rupp-135">[132]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In Japan, the romantic "date night" associated to Valentine's Day is celebrated on <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Eve" title="Christmas Eve">Christmas Eve</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[136]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lebanon">Lebanon</span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Valentine%27s_day_cupcakes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Valentine%27s_day_cupcakes.jpg/170px-Valentine%27s_day_cupcakes.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Valentine%27s_day_cupcakes.jpg/255px-Valentine%27s_day_cupcakes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Valentine%27s_day_cupcakes.jpg/340px-Valentine%27s_day_cupcakes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="960"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Valentine%27s_day_cupcakes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Valentine's Day themed bouquet of <a href="/wiki/Cupcake" title="Cupcake">cupcakes</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Saint_Valentine" title="Saint Valentine">Saint Valentine</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saint</a> for a large part of the <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanese</a> population. Couples take the opportunity of Valentine's feast day to exchange sweet words and gifts as proof of love. Such gifts typically include boxes of chocolates, cupcakes, and red roses, which are considered the emblem of sacrifice and passion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanese</a> people celebrate Valentine's Day in a different way in every city. In <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>, men take women out to dine and may buy them a gift. Many women are asked to marry on that day. In <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>, Valentine's Day is celebrated with the whole family – it is more about family love than a couple's love.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia">Malaysia</span></h4> 
      <p>Islamic officials in <a href="/wiki/West_Malaysia" class="mw-redirect" title="West Malaysia">West Malaysia</a> warned Muslims against celebrating Valentine's Day, linking it with vice activities. Deputy Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Muhyiddin_Yassin" title="Muhyiddin Yassin">Muhyiddin Yassin</a> said the celebration of romantic love was "not suitable" for Muslims. Wan Mohamad Sheikh Abdul Aziz, head of the Malaysian Islamic Development Department (<a href="/wiki/JAKIM#Official_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="JAKIM">Jakim</a>), which oversees the country's Islamic policies said that a <a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwa</a> (ruling) issued by the country's top clerics in 2005 noted that the day 'is associated with elements of Christianity,' and 'we just cannot get involved with other religions' worshipping <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">rituals</a>.' Jakim officials planned to carry out a nationwide campaign called "Awas Jerat Valentine's Day" ("Mind the Valentine's Day Trap"), aimed at preventing Muslims from celebrating the day on February 14, 2011. Activities include conducting raids in hotels to stop young couples from having unlawful sex and distributing leaflets to Muslim university students warning them against the day.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On Valentine's Day 2011, West Malaysian religious authorities arrested more than 100 Muslim couples concerning the celebration ban. Some of them would be charged in the Shariah Court for defying the department's ban against the celebration of Valentine's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[139]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/East_Malaysia" title="East Malaysia">East Malaysia</a>, the celebration are much more tolerated among young Muslim couples although some Islamic officials and Muslim activists from the West side have told younger generations to refrain from such celebration by organising da'wah and tried to spread their ban into the East.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup> In both the states of <a href="/wiki/Sabah" title="Sabah">Sabah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarawak" title="Sarawak">Sarawak</a>, the celebration is usually common with flowers.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day_in_Pakistan" title="Valentine's Day in Pakistan">Valentine's Day in Pakistan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The concept of Valentine's Day was introduced into Pakistan during the late 1990s with special TV and radio programs. The <a href="/wiki/Jamaat-e-Islami_Pakistan" title="Jamaat-e-Islami Pakistan">Jamaat-e-Islami</a> political party has called for the banning of Valentine's Day celebration.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcanger_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcanger-117">[114]</a></sup> Despite this, the celebration is becoming popular among urban youth and the florists expect to sell a great amount of flowers, especially red roses. The case is the same with card publishers.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2016, local governing body of <a href="/wiki/Peshwar" class="mw-redirect" title="Peshwar">Peshwar</a> officially banned the celebration of Valentine's Day in the city. The ban was also implemented in other cities such as <a href="/wiki/Kohat" title="Kohat">Kohat</a> by the local governments.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[146]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Islamabad_High_Court" title="Islamabad High Court">Islamabad High Court</a> banned Valentine's Day celebrations in public places in Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[147]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span></h4> 
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, Valentine's Day is called <i>Araw ng mga Puso</i> in much the same manner as in the West. It is usually marked by a steep increase in the price of flowers, particularly red roses.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[148]</a></sup> It is the most popular day for weddings,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup> with some localities offering mass ceremonies for no charge.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[150]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Saudi_Arabia">Saudi Arabia</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, in 2002 and 2008, <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_the_Promotion_of_Virtue_and_the_Prevention_of_Vice_(Saudi_Arabia)" title="Committee for the Promotion of Virtue and the Prevention of Vice (Saudi Arabia)">religious police</a> banned the sale of all Valentine's Day items, telling shop workers to remove any red items, because the day is considered a Christian holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_2002_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_2002-154">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc_saudi_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_saudi-155">[152]</a></sup> This ban has created a <a href="/wiki/Black_market" title="Black market">black market</a> for <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">roses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wrapping_paper" class="mw-redirect" title="Wrapping paper">wrapping paper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_saudi_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_saudi-155">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LA_Times_2010_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LA_Times_2010-156">[153]</a></sup> In 2012, the religious police arrested more than 140 Muslims for celebrating the holiday, and confiscated all red roses from flower shops.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup> Muslims are not allowed to celebrate the holiday, and non-Muslims can celebrate only behind closed doors.<sup id="cite_ref-saudi_2013_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-saudi_2013-158">[155]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>"Saudi cleric Sheikh Muhammad Al-'Arifi said on Valentine's Day Eve that celebrating this holiday constitutes <a href="/wiki/Bid%27a" class="mw-redirect" title="Bid'a">bid'a</a> – a forbidden innovation and deviation from religious law and custom – and mimicry of the West."<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[157]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>However, in 2017 and 2018, after a <a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwa</a> was widely circulated, the religious police did not prevent Muslims from celebrating the day.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[158]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span></h4> 
      <p>According to findings, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singaporeans</a> are among the biggest spenders on Valentine's Day, with 60% of Singaporeans indicating that they would spend between $100 and $500 during the season leading up to the holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-showbizandstyle.inquirer.net_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-showbizandstyle.inquirer.net-92">[89]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Korea">South Korea</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, women give chocolate to men on February 14, and men give non-chocolate candy to women on March 14 (<a href="/wiki/White_Day" title="White Day">White Day</a>). On April 14 (Black Day), those who did not receive anything on February or March 14 go to a Chinese-Korean restaurant to eat black noodles (짜장면 <i><a href="/wiki/Jajangmyeon" title="Jajangmyeon">jajangmyeon</a></i>) and lament their 'single life'.<sup id="cite_ref-yoshimura_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoshimura-137">[134]</a></sup> Koreans also celebrate <a href="/wiki/Pepero_Day" title="Pepero Day">Pepero Day</a> on November 11, when young couples give each other Pepero cookies. The date '11/11' is intended to resemble the long shape of the cookie. The 14th of every month marks a love-related day in Korea, although most of them are obscure. From January to December: Candle Day, Valentine's Day, White Day, <a href="/wiki/Black_Day_(South_Korea)" title="Black Day (South Korea)">Black Day</a>, Rose Day, Kiss Day, Silver Day, Green Day, Music Day, Wine Day, Movie Day, and Hug Day.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup> Korean women give a much higher amount of chocolate than Japanese women.<sup id="cite_ref-yoshimura_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoshimura-137">[134]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Taiwan">Taiwan</span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:TAIPEI_101_in_Valentine%27s_Day.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/TAIPEI_101_in_Valentine%27s_Day.JPG/220px-TAIPEI_101_in_Valentine%27s_Day.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/TAIPEI_101_in_Valentine%27s_Day.JPG/330px-TAIPEI_101_in_Valentine%27s_Day.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/TAIPEI_101_in_Valentine%27s_Day.JPG/440px-TAIPEI_101_in_Valentine%27s_Day.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:TAIPEI_101_in_Valentine%27s_Day.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Taipei_101" title="Taipei 101">Taipei 101</a> in Valentine's Day 2006
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of China">Taiwan</a>, traditional <a href="/wiki/Qixi_Festival" title="Qixi Festival">Qixi Festival</a>, Valentine's Day and White Day are all celebrated. However, the situation is the reverse of Japan's. Men give gifts to women on Valentine's Day, and women return them on <a href="/wiki/White_Day" title="White Day">White Day</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-yoshimura_137-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoshimura-137">[134]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span></h4> 
      <p>In the UK, just under half of the population spend money on their Valentines and around £1.3&nbsp;billion is spent yearly on cards, flowers, chocolates, and other gifts, with an estimated 25 million cards being sent.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In Wales, some people celebrate <a href="/wiki/Dydd_Santes_Dwynwen" title="Dydd Santes Dwynwen">Dydd Santes Dwynwen</a> (St. Dwynwen's Day) on January 25 instead of (or as well as) Valentine's Day. The day commemorates <a href="/wiki/Dwynwen" title="Dwynwen">St Dwynwen</a>, the Welsh patron saint of love.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[161]</a></sup> The Welsh name for Saint Valentine is Sant Ffolant. </p>
      <p>In a 2016 poll conducted by <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a> for Valentine's Day, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a>’s line, “My heart is, and always will be, yours”, from her novel <i><a href="/wiki/Sense_and_Sensibility" title="Sense and Sensibility">Sense and Sensibility</a></i> and said by Edward Ferrars (<a href="/wiki/Hugh_Grant" title="Hugh Grant">Hugh Grant</a>) to Elinor Dashwood (<a href="/wiki/Emma_Thompson" title="Emma Thompson">Emma Thompson</a>) in the acclaimed <a href="/wiki/Sense_and_Sensibility_(film)" title="Sense and Sensibility (film)">1995 film adaptation</a>, was voted the most romantic line from literature, film and TV by thousands of women.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[162]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church.JPG/220px-Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church.JPG/330px-Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church.JPG/440px-Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3072"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Many Christians make a <a href="/wiki/Christian_pilgrimage" title="Christian pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a> to <a href="/wiki/Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church" title="Whitefriar Street Carmelite Church">Whitefriar Street Carmelite Church</a> in Dublin on Saint Valentine's Day to implore the <a href="/wiki/Intercession_of_saints" title="Intercession of saints">intercession</a> of Saint Valentine in their <a href="/wiki/Christian_prayer" title="Christian prayer">prayers</a>, with the hope of finding true love<sup id="cite_ref-IrishCentral2017_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IrishCentral2017-166">[163]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On Saint Valentine's Day in Ireland, many individuals who seek true love make a <a href="/wiki/Christian_pilgrimage" title="Christian pilgrimage">Christian pilgrimage</a> to the Shrine of St. Valentine in <a href="/wiki/Whitefriar_Street_Carmelite_Church" title="Whitefriar Street Carmelite Church">Whitefriar Street Carmelite Church</a> in Dublin, which is said to house relics of Saint Valentine of Rome; they <a href="/wiki/Christian_prayer" title="Christian prayer">pray</a> at the shrine in hope of finding romance.<sup id="cite_ref-IrishCentral2017_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IrishCentral2017-166">[163]</a></sup> There lies a book in which foreigners and locals have written their prayer requests for love.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[164]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland_and_Estonia">Finland and Estonia</span></h4> 
      <p>In Finland, Valentine's Day is called <i>ystävänpäivä</i>, which means "Friend's Day". As the name indicates, this day is more about remembering friends, not significant others. In Estonia, Valentine's Day was originally called <i>valentinipäev</i> and later also <i>sõbrapäev</i> ('Friend's Day') as a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> of the Finnish term.<sup id="cite_ref-s6brap2ev_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s6brap2ev-168">[165]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span></h4> 
      <p>In France, a traditionally <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> country, Valentine's Day is known simply as "<a href="/wiki/Saint_Valentine" title="Saint Valentine">Saint Valentin</a>", and is celebrated in much the same way as other western countries.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[166]</a></sup> The relics of Saint Valentin de Terni, the patron of the St Valentine's Day, are in the Catholic church of Saint-Jean-Baptiste and Saint-Jean-l’Evangéliste located in the southern France town of <a href="/wiki/Roquemaure,_Gard" title="Roquemaure, Gard">Roquemaure, Gard</a>. The celebrations of "Fête des Amoureux" takes place every two years on the Sunday closest to February 14. The village gets dressed in its 19th-century costume and put on the program with over 800 people. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span></h4> 
      <p>St. Valentine's Day, or Ημέρα του Αγίου Βαλεντίνου in Greek tradition was not associated with romantic love. In the Eastern Orthodox church there is another Saint who protects people who are in love, <a href="/wiki/Hyacinth_of_Caesarea" title="Hyacinth of Caesarea">Hyacinth of Caesarea</a> (feast day July 3), but this was not widely known until the late 1990s<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[167]</a></sup> In contemporary Greece, Valentine's Day is generally celebrated as in the common Western tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[168]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland">Poland</span></h4> 
      <p>Saint Valentine's Day was introduced to Poland together with the cult of Saint Valentine via <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tyrol" title="Tyrol">Tyrol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-172">[169]</a></sup> However, it rose in popularity only in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-172">[169]</a></sup> The only (and the biggest) public celebration in Poland is held annually from 2002 in <a href="/wiki/Che%C5%82mno" title="Chełmno">Chełmno</a><sup id="cite_ref-:0_172-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-172">[169]</a></sup> under the name „Walentynki Chełmińskie” (Chełmno Valentine's). Because Chełmno's parish church of the Assumption of the Blessed Virgin Mary has been holding the <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relic</a> of St. Valentine since the Middle Ages, local cult of the saint has been combined with the Anglo-Saxon tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_172-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-172">[169]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, the holiday is known as "Dia dos Namorados" (Lover's Day / Day of the Enamoured). As elsewhere, couples exchange gifts, but in some regions, women give a <i>lenço de namorados</i> ("lovers' handkerchief"), which is usually embroidered with love motifs.<sup id="cite_ref-Poelzl2009_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poelzl2009-173">[170]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Romania">Romania</span></h4> 
      <p>In recent years, Romania has also started celebrating Valentine's Day. This has drawn backlash from several groups, institutions,<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[171]</a></sup> and nationalist organizations like <a href="/wiki/Noua_Dreapta" class="mw-redirect" title="Noua Dreapta">Noua Dreaptǎ</a>, who condemn Valentine's Day for being superficial, commercialist, and imported Western <a href="/wiki/Kitsch" title="Kitsch">kitsch</a>. In order to counter the perceived denaturation of national culture, <a href="/wiki/Dragobete" title="Dragobete">Dragobete</a>, a spring festival celebrated in parts of Southern Romania, has been rekindled after having been ignored during the Communist years as the traditional Romanian holiday for lovers. The holiday is named after a character from Romanian folklore who was supposed to be the son of <a href="/wiki/Baba_Dochia" title="Baba Dochia">Baba Dochia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[172]</a></sup> Its date used to vary depending on the geographical area, however nowadays it is commonly observed on February 24.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[173]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, February 14 is known as <i>Valentinsdag</i>, and it is celebrated in much the same manner as in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Skandiblog_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skandiblog-177">[174]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> it is called <i>Alla hjärtans dag</i> ("All Hearts' Day") but is not widely celebrated. A 2016 survey revealed that less than 50% of men and women were planning to buy presents for their partners.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[175]</a></sup> The holiday has only been observed since the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-Skandiblog_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skandiblog-177">[174]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span></h4> 
      <p>In Spain, Valentine's Day is known as "San Valentín" and is celebrated the same way as in the rest of the West. </p> 
      <h2><span id="Valentine.27s_Day_in_popular_culture"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Valentine's_Day_in_popular_culture">Valentine's Day in popular culture</span></h2> 
      <p>Many films have been produced depicting various aspects of Valentine's Day, including <i><a href="/wiki/A_Charlie_Brown_Valentine" title="A Charlie Brown Valentine">A Charlie Brown Valentine</a></i> (2002). </p> 
      <h2><span id="Antivalentinism_and_banning_of_Valentine.27s_day_in_some_countries"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Antivalentinism_and_banning_of_Valentine's_day_in_some_countries">Antivalentinism and banning of Valentine's day in some countries</span></h2> 
      <p>Pakistan is the latest country to ban Valentine’s Day celebrations in public spaces after the country’s High Court passed a ruling. Before that, Officials and clerics in Indonesia have banned and rejected the idea of Valentine’s Day saying they it is not part of Islamic culture. Protestors holding boards saying “Muslims say no to Happy Valentine’s Day” held rallies in four cities in Indonesia.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[176]</a></sup> Since 2009, certain practices pertaining to Valentine's Day (such as giving flowers, cards, or other gifts suggestive of Valentine's Day) are banned in Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Law_Enforcement_Force_of_the_Islamic_Republic_of_Iran" title="Law Enforcement Force of the Islamic Republic of Iran">Law Enforcement Force</a> fight with distributors of goods with symbols associated with Valentine's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[178]</a></sup> In 2021, prosecution of Qom declared that it will fight with those who disseminate and provide anti-cultural symbols like those of Valentine's day.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[179]</a></sup> Although Valentine's Day is not accepted/approved by any <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institution</a> or rule in the country and has no official status, it's highly accepted among a large part of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[180]</a></sup> One of the reasons for Valentine's Day breaking out into the general population in Iran is the change in relations between the sexes, which are no longer bound to marriage for two decades.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[181]</a></sup> </p>

      </ul> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style>
      <div class="reflist"> 
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references"> 
         <li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, one source claims incorrectly that "Pope Gelasius I muddled things in the 5th century by combining St. Valentine's Day with Lupercalia to expel the pagan rituals." Seipel, Arnie, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.npr.org/2011/02/14/133693152/the-dark-origins-of-valentines-day"><i>The Dark Origins Of Valentine's Day</i></a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160427232033/http://www.npr.org/2011/02/14/133693152/the-dark-origins-of-valentines-day">Archived</a> April 27, 2016, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, Nation Public Radio, February 13, 2011</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ansgar, 1976, pp. 60–61. The replacement of Lupercalia with Saint Valentine's celebration was suggested by researchers Kellog and Cox. Ansgar says "It is hardly credible, then, that Pope Gelasius could have introduced the feast of the Purification to counteract the Lupercalia, and in fact the historical records of his pontificate give no hint of such an action."</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ansgar, 1976, pp. 60–61. This feast is celebrated 40 days after the Nativity. In Jerusalem the Nativity was celebrated on January 6, and this feast in February 14. But, in the West and even in Eastern places such as Antioch and Alexandria, Nativity was celebrated on December 25, and this Purification was not celebrated. When this feast was introduced to Rome, it was directly placed in February 2. Around that time, Jerusalem adopted the Nativity date of December 25 and moved the Purification to February 2.</span> </li> 
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>

Valentine, a physician priest, offered help to Christians whose lives were in peril and, although celibate himself, performed secret marriage rites for young men and women, defying the emperor's decree.          <li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Henry Ansgar Kelly, in <i>Chaucer and the Cult of Saint Valentine</i> (Leiden: Brill) 1986, accounts for these and further local Saints Valentine (Ch. 6 "The Genoese Saint Valentine and the observances of May") in arguing that Chaucer had an established tradition in mind, and (pp. 79 ff.) linking the Valentine in question to Valentine, first bishop of Genoa, the only Saint Valentine honoured with a feast in springtime, the season indicated by Chaucer. Valentine of Genoa was treated by <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_de_Voragine" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacobus de Voragine">Jacobus of Verazze</a> in his <i>Chronicle of Genoa</i> (Kelly p. 85).</span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Oxford Dictionary of Saints</i>, <i>s.v.</i> "Valentine": "The <a href="/wiki/Acta_Sanctorum" title="Acta Sanctorum">Acts</a> of both are unreliable, and the Bollandists assert that these two Valentines were in fact one and the same."</span> </li>

         <li id="cite_note-oruch-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-oruch_29-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-oruch_29-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-oruch_29-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-oruch_29-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-oruch_29-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-oruch_29-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Oruch, Jack B., "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/2847741">St. Valentine, Chaucer, and Spring in February</a>", <i>Speculum</i>, <b>56</b> (1981): 534–65. Oruch's survey of the literature finds no association between Valentine and romance prior to Chaucer.          <li id="cite_note-Brewer1894-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Brewer1894_31-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFBrewer1894" class="citation book cs1">Brewer, Ebenezer Cobham (1894). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=blUXAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PA384"><i>A Dictionary of Miracles: Imitative, Realistic, and Dogmatic</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/J._B._Lippincott_%26_Co." title="J. B. Lippincott &amp; Co.">J. B. Lippincott &amp; Co.</a> p.&nbsp;384. <q>St. Valentine, laying his hand upon her eyes, said in prayer, "O Thou who art the true Light, give light to this Thy servant." Instantly sight was restored to the blind child. Asterius and his wife, falling at the feet of Valentine, prayed that they might be admitted into the Christian fellowship; whereupon St. Valentine commanded them to break their idols, to fast for three days, to forgive their enemies, and to be baptized. Asterius and his wife did all the saint told them to do, and Valentine baptized them and all their household, to the number of forty-six in all. —<i>Les Petits Bollandistes</i>, vol. ii. pp. 510, 511.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Dictionary+of+Miracles%3A+Imitative%2C+Realistic%2C+and+Dogmatic&amp;rft.pages=384&amp;rft.pub=J.+B.+Lippincott+%26+Co.&amp;rft.date=1894&amp;rft.aulast=Brewer&amp;rft.aufirst=Ebenezer+Cobham&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DblUXAAAAYAAJ%26pg%3DPA384&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AValentine%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li>

         <li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.history.com/this-day-in-history/st-valentine-beheaded">"St. Valentine beheaded – Feb 14, 278"</a>. <a href="/wiki/History_(U.S._TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="History (U.S. TV channel)">History</a>. February 14, 2012. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150316200003/http://www.history.com/this-day-in-history/st-valentine-beheaded">Archived</a> from the original on March 16, 2015<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">April 9,</span> 2015</span>. <q>When Valentine's actions were discovered, Claudius ordered that he be put to death. Valentine was arrested and dragged before the Prefect of Rome, who condemned him to be beaten to death with clubs and to have his head cut off. The sentence was carried out on February 14, on or about the year 270. Legend also has it that while in jail, St. Valentine left a farewell note for the jailer's daughter, who had become his friend, and signed it "From Your Valentine."</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=St.+Valentine+beheaded+%E2%80%93+Feb+14%2C+278&amp;rft.pub=History&amp;rft.date=2012-02-14&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.history.com%2Fthis-day-in-history%2Fst-valentine-beheaded&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AValentine%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li>

         <li id="cite_note-BDForbes2015-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-BDForbes2015_50-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BDForbes2015_50-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFForbes2015" class="citation book cs1">Forbes, Bruce David (October 27, 2015). <i>America's Favorite Holidays: Candid Histories</i>. <a href="/wiki/University_of_California_Press" title="University of California Press">University of California Press</a>. p.&nbsp;54. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780520284722" title="Special:BookSources/9780520284722"><bdi>9780520284722</bdi></a>. <q>There is no indication in suppressing the Lupercalia, Gelasius put anything else in its place. Much later, in the 1500s, a Cardinal Baronius speculated that Gelasius converted the Lupercalia into the Feast of the Purification of the Virgin (or Candlemas), changing one purification ceremony into another, and many noted authors have repeated this claim. Recent scholarship has refuted Baronius's assertion...there is no evidence that Gelasius advocated a celebration of Valentine's Day as a replacement for the Lupercalia. ... The letter by Gelasius to Andromachus criticizing the Lupercalia contains no reference to Valentine, or Valentine's Day, or any replacement observance.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=America%27s+Favorite+Holidays%3A+Candid+Histories&amp;rft.pages=54&amp;rft.pub=University+of+California+Press&amp;rft.date=2015-10-27&amp;rft.isbn=9780520284722&amp;rft.aulast=Forbes&amp;rft.aufirst=Bruce+David&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AValentine%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li>
